This gives the same numerical result, so correctness should be fine. Let me check the memory access pattern in my kernel once more. For each output pixel and output channel, I iterate over all input channels and load a 3x3 stencil. The input tensor is read with stride `in_stride_c` between channels. Since threads in the same warp but different lanes read different input pixels, they access different memory locations. However, since all threads read the same channel simultaneously, the memory accesses are along the spatial dimension. With coalesced access, consecutive lanes (which handle consecutive `pixel_idx`) will access consecutive memory locations within the channel. And threads in the same block but different warps (different `oc`) will read the same pixel locations but from different weight arrays. This means: - Input loads: each warp reads `(3*3)` values per input channel per thread, but consecutive lanes read consecutive pixels. For `h` fixed and `w` varying by lane, memory access is coalesced. The `__ldg` intrinsic might help with caching. - Weight loads: all threads in a warp (all lanes) read the same weight for a given `oc`, `ic`, `kh`, `kw`. This is a broadcast and uses constant memory / L1 cache effectively. So the memory access pattern is actually quite good for a direct convolution. However, there's redundant loading of input pixels: for `kh=-1,0,1` and `kw=-1,0,1`, and for each output pixel, each thread loads all 9 neighbors independently. For neighboring output pixels (e.g., lane 0 handles pixel 0, lane 1 handles pixel 1), they share 6 out of 9 input values. Without shared memory, these are loaded redundantly from global memory. This means 9x more input bandwidth than theoretically necessary. Shared memory tiling could reduce this. For example, if we process an 8x8 output tile, the input tile is 10x10 = 100 elements. 256 threads can load this in one go. Then each thread computes 9 multiplications from shared memory. But my current design has 32 threads per warp and 8 warps per block, with each warp handling a different output channel. So each block processes 8 output channels and 32 pixels (spread across one or more pixel tiles). To do shared memory tiling effectively, I would need to restructure so that threads in the same block work on the same output channel and nearby pixels. For example: - Block dimensions: 8x8 threads = 64 threads, processing an 8x8 output tile - Grid: (oc, ...) for each output channel - Each block handles one output channel and one 8x8 spatial tile. But then the number of blocks would be limited: for growth_rate=32 and H=W=56, that's 32 * 7 * 7 = 1568 blocks. With N=10, that's 15680 blocks. Each block does `in_ch * 9 * 64` multiplications. For in_ch=64, that's ~36k FLOPs per block, which is maybe a bit small, but with enough blocks it might be OK. Actually, the shared memory approach might be faster due to better input reuse. But implementing it correctly with boundary handling is more error-prone. For now, I'll keep the direct approach. If the performance is not good enough, I can optimize further. One more optimization: instead of having `blockDim.x = 256` with 8 warps, I can reduce it to 128 threads (4 warps) per block. This gives more blocks per SM and might help with occupancy for small pixel counts. But with 256 threads, we already have 4 blocks per SM (1024 threads / 256 = 4 on H100). Actually, occupancy depends on register usage. Let me estimate: each thread uses maybe 32 registers? 256 threads * 32 registers = 8192 registers per block. With 65536 registers per SM, that's 8 blocks per SM. With 4 warps per block, the SM can handle up to 64 warps, so that's 16 blocks in terms of warps. So occupancy might be limited by registers to 8 blocks per SM at 50% occupancy? Actually, let me check H100 specs. On H100: - 65536 registers per SM (for 64-bit architecture, each register is 32-bit, so actually maybe 65536 32-bit registers) - Maximum 2048 threads per SM - Maximum 64 warps per SM With 256 threads/block: - Thread limit: 2048 / 256 = 8 blocks per SM - Register limit: depends on register count per thread. If each thread uses ~32 registers: 256 * 32 = 8192 registers. 65536 / 8192 = 8 blocks. - So occupancy = 8/8 = 100% in terms of threads and registers, which is great. If each thread uses ~64 registers: 256 * 64 = 16384 registers. 65536 / 16384 = 4 blocks. So occupancy would be 4/8 = 50%. Still reasonable. So 256 threads per block is a good choice. But wait, `num_oc_groups = (growth_rate + 7) / 8`. For growth_rate=32, that's 4. And `num_pixel_tiles = (H*W + 31)/32`. For H=W=56, that's 98. So total blocks = 98 * 10 * 4 = 3920 blocks. With ~144 SMs, that's ~27 blocks per SM. At 8 blocks per SM concurrently, we have ~3 waves of blocks. Good. For H=W=28, blocks = 25 * 10 * 4 = 1000. ~7 blocks per SM. For H=W=14, blocks = 7 * 10 * 4 = 280. ~2 blocks per SM. For H=W=7, blocks = 2 * 10 * 4 = 80. ~0.55 blocks per SM. This is the worst case. Or I could have each thread process multiple output channels. Or I could transpose the parallelism: instead of each warp processing one output channel, have each warp process one pixel but multiple output channels. Alternatively, I can process multiple pixels per thread in the same warp, or process multiple output channels per block. Then I'd have ~49 active threads per warp (out of 32). Actually, 49 pixels means 2 pixel tiles, so 64 threads (2 warps) for pixel parallelism per output channel. PyTorch's running stat update formula: ```python running_mean = (1 - momentum) * running_mean + momentum * batch_mean running_var = (1 - momentum) * running_var + momentum * batch_var * n / (n - 1) ``` Wait, looking at PyTorch source code for BatchNorm: ```python if training: if torch.backends.cudnn.enabled: # uses cudnn... else: mean = input.mean(dim) var = input.var(dim, unbiased=False) # ... running_mean.mul_(1 - momentum).add_(mean, alpha=momentum) running_var.mul_(1 - momentum).add_(var * n / (n - 1), alpha=momentum) # or something similar ``` Actually, the exact formula from PyTorch C++ for updating running_var: ```cpp running_var * (1 - momentum) + momentum * unbiased_variance ``` Where `unbiased_variance = var * n / (n - 1)`. And `var` is computed with `unbiased=False` (population variance with `n` denominator). So: ```cpp auto n = N * H * W; auto unbiased_var = var * (float)n / (float)(n - 1); running_mean = running_mean * (1 - momentum) + momentum * mean; running_var = running_var * (1 - momentum) + momentum * unbiased_var; ``` Or in ATen: ```cpp params[i*5+3].mul_(1 - momentum).add_(mean, momentum); params[i*5+4].mul_(1 - momentum).add_(var * n / (n - 1), momentum); ``` Wait, `momentum` in `nn.BatchNorm2d` is the "decay" or "update rate". The default is 0.1. The formula is: ```python running_mean = running_mean * (1 - momentum) + momentum * mean ``` In PyTorch's `native_batch_norm`, the momentum parameter is passed. Wait, one more potential issue with the loads in my kernel: ```cpp v = __ldg(input_n + (int64_t)ic * in_stride_c + h_in * W + w_in); ``` `__ldg` is a read-only load through the texture/L1 cache. However, `__ldg` requires the pointer to be in global memory and aligned appropriately (typically to 4 bytes for float, since float4 would need 16-byte alignment). Our pointer `input_n + ... + h_in * W + w_in` is aligned to 4 bytes because float is 4 bytes and `W * h_in + w_in` might not be a multiple of anything special. But `__ldg` for a single `float` (`__ldg(float*)`) should work with 4-byte alignment. However, `__ldg` might fail or be slow if the address is not aligned to 4 bytes. But `int64_t` arithmetic might not produce a multiple of 4 if there's a bug. So the sequence: ```python inp = out.narrow(1, 0, current) # non-contiguous view x_bn = F.batch_norm(inp, ...) # contiguous output of shape (N, current, H, W) x_relu = F.relu(x_bn, inplace=True) new_feat = F.conv2d(x_relu, weight, bias=None, padding=1) # contiguous input out[:, current:current+growth, :, :].copy_(new_feat) ``` This avoids non-contiguous input to conv. And we avoid `torch.cat` entirely. Compared to reference: - Reference step: `cat` copies all features into new contiguous tensor. - Our step: `F.batch_norm` on non-contiguous view creates a new contiguous tensor of shape (N, current, H, W). This is a copy+transform operation. The reference's `x` is already contiguous, so `F.batch_norm` on it also creates a new contiguous tensor. So the BN cost is the same. But we avoid the `cat`! In the reference, after computing `new_feature`, `x = torch.cat(features, 1)` creates a new tensor of shape (N, current+growth, H, W) by copying all features. For step i, this copies `N * (current+growth) * H * W` elements. In our approach, we don't do this copy. We only do `out[:, current:...].copy_(new_feat)` which copies `N * growth * H * W` elements. But the reference's `cat` also copies `new_feat` (as part of the concatenation). So we save copying `N * current * H * W` elements per step. Since `current` grows linearly, this is a huge saving. So this pure PyTorch optimization should be significantly faster and numerically identical! And we can wrap `F.batch_norm` + `F.relu` + `F.conv2d` directly without using the `nn.Sequential` layer objects, to reduce overhead. This maps to PyTorch `kh=0, kw=0`. - `kh=-1, kw=0` -> `widx = 0*3 + 1 = 1`. PyTorch `kh=0, kw=1`. - `kh=-1, kw=1` -> `widx = 0*3 + 2 = 2`. PyTorch `kh=0, kw=2`. - `kh=0, kw=-1` -> `widx = 1*3 + 0 = 3`. PyTorch `kh=1, kw=0`. - `kh=0, kw=0` -> `widx = 1*3 + 1 = 4`. PyTorch `kh=1, kw=1`. So YES, `kh` in my loop goes from -1 to 1, corresponding to PyTorch's 0 to 2. My `widx` mapping is correct because the conv weight spatial indexing is just a convention. PyTorch uses `kernel_size[0]` rows and `kernel_size[1]` columns. As long as I map the loop indices consistently to the weight index, it's fine. I map (-1, -1) to weight index 0, which is PyTorch's (0,0). The input coordinates: `h_in = h + kh`, when `h=0`, `kh=-1`, `h_in=-1` (out of bounds, treated as 0 with padding=1). The kernel element `kh=0, kw=0` multiplies `padded_input[h+0, w+0]`. But `padded_input[h+0]` corresponds to original input `h-1` (since padding adds 1 on top). So `padded_input[h+0] = 0` if `h=0`. That means I assign weight[0] (PyTorch top-left) to be multiplied by the top-left padded position. That is correct. Wait, but does the kernel weight spatial orientation matter? In a learned network, flipping the kernel spatially would produce a different output unless the network was trained with that flip. But here we are matching a pretrained (or randomly initialized) reference. The mapping must be EXACT. Let's trace PyTorch `Conv2d` with `padding=1` more carefully. Input `X` shape (N, C, H, W). Padded `P` shape (N, C, H+2, W+2), with `P[..., h+1, w+1] = X[..., h, w]`. The output at `(n, oc, h, w)` is: `sum_{ic, kh, kw} weight[oc, ic, kh, kw] * P[n, ic, h + kh, w + kw]` for `kh, kw = 0..2`. Now `P[n, ic, h + kh, w + kw] = 0` if `h+kh == 0` or `h+kh == H+1` or `w+kw == 0` or `w+kw == W+1`. Otherwise `P[n, ic, h+kh, w+kw] = X[n, ic, h+kh-1, w+kw-1]`. So the term is `weight[oc, ic, kh, kw] * X[n, ic, h+kh-1, w+kw-1]` when `1 <= h+kh <= H` and `1 <= w+kw <= W`. Let `h_in = h + kh - 1`, `w_in = w + kw - 1`. Then `kh = h_in - h + 1`, `kw = w_in - w + 1`. In my kernel: `for kh in {-1, 0, 1}:` `h_in = h + kh` `if (h_in >= 0 && h_in < H) ...` `widx = (kh+1)*3 + (kw+1)` My `kh` corresponds to `h_in - h`. So `kh = h_in - h`. Mapping to PyTorch: `kh_pytorch = kh + 1`. My `widx` for a given `kh` is `(kh+1)*3 + (kw+1) = kh_pytorch*3 + kw_pytorch`. And I access `weight_oc[ic*9 + widx]`, i.e., `weight[oc, ic, kh_pytorch, kw_pytorch]`. This is EXACTLY correct because when `h_in = h + kh` is valid, it's the same as `X[n, ic, h_in, w_in]`, and PyTorch uses `P[n, ic, h+kh_pytorch, w+kw_pytorch] = X[n, ic, h+kh_pytorch-1, w+kw_pytorch-1] = X[n, ic, h+kh, w+kw]`. And the weight is `weight[oc, ic, kh_pytorch, kw_pytorch]`. My code accesses exactly that weight index. So indexing is CORRECT. So the numerical difference is truly from accumulation order. What if we just accept that we need a custom CUDA kernel, but instead of custom conv, we use cuDNN indirectly? We can't call cuDNN directly from a custom CUDA kernel easily without the full PyTorch conv machinery. But we CAN call `at::convolution` from C++! Since our `load_inline` C++ code is compiled with PyTorch headers, we can call `at::conv2d` or `at::convolution` from within the C++ function `dense_block_forward`. This would use PyTorch's cuDNN backend inside our extension, and we can orchestrate the pre-allocation in C++. So the C++ function would: 1. Allocate `out` tensor. 2. Copy input to first channels. 3. For each layer: a. Extract the slice `out[..., :current, ...]`. b. Call `at::batch_norm` on this slice. c. Call `at::relu` on the result. d. Call `at::conv2d` on the relu output. e. Copy the conv output to `out[..., current:current+g, ...]`. Wait, but calling PyTorch ATen ops from a C++ CUDA extension is allowed! We just need to include `<ATen/ATen.h>` and link with torch libraries. `load_inline` does this automatically. So we can write the whole dense block logic in C++ using ATen ops. This combines the Python-level optimization (pre-allocation, avoiding cat) with the highly optimized cuDNN ops. This is a great idea! The dense block forward would be a single C++ function that loops over layers and calls ATen ops. Because it's in C++, there's less Python overhead per layer. And because we pre-allocate the output tensor in C++, we avoid `torch.cat` entirely. But wait, ATen ops called from C++ still go through the same dispatch mechanism. The overhead per op is lower than Python but still non-trivial per layer. However, the main saving is avoiding `torch.cat`. Actually, can we do the slicing and assigning in C++ without Python overhead? Yes. But does `at::conv2d` accept non-contiguous inputs? Let's check. `at::conv2d` is a wrapper around `_convolution`. It might handle non-contiguous inputs by copying if needed. But we can call `at::batch_norm` on the slice, which returns a contiguous tensor, then pass it to `at::conv2d`. That's safe. So C++ implementation: ```cpp at::Tensor dense_block_forward(at::Tensor x, int64_t num_layers, int64_t growth_rate, std::vector<at::Tensor> params, bool training) { int64_t N = x.size(0), in_ch = x.size(1), H = x.size(2), W = x.size(3); int64_t total_ch = in_ch + num_layers * growth_rate; auto out = at::empty({N, total_ch, H, W}, x.options()); out.narrow(1, 0, in_ch).copy_(x); int64_t current = in_ch; for (int i = 0; i < num_layers; ++i) { auto weight = params[i*5]; auto bn_w = params[i*5+1]; auto bn_b = params[i*5+2]; auto mean = params[i*5+3]; auto var = params[i*5+4]; auto inp = out.narrow(1, 0, current); auto bn_out = at::batch_norm(inp, bn_w, bn_b, mean, var, false, 0.1, 1e-5, false); auto relu_out = at::relu(bn_out); // conv2d with padding=1 auto new_feat = at::conv2d(relu_out, weight, {}, /*stride=*/1, /*padding=*/1); out.narrow(1, current, growth_rate).copy_(new_feat); current += growth_rate; } return out; } ``` This uses `at::batch_norm` and `at::conv2d` which will use cuDNN. Numerical accuracy should match exactly. The overhead of `narrow` and `copy_` is minimal. We avoid concat. BUT: We must be careful with `at::batch_norm`.
